\begin{lemma}[Adjacent support-ordering]
\label{lem:adjacent-support-ordering}

Insert this LaTeX

\begin{lemma}[Compact adjacent pair-pair implies a directional kernel zero]
\label{lem:pair-pair-gives-directional-kernel-zero}
Let $I_1<I_2$ be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary,
and let
\[
L:\ y=mx+b
\]
be a finite-slope line that is $2$-secant to both $\Gamma(I_1)$ and
$\Gamma(I_2)$.
Then, after removing a sufficiently small boundary neighborhood, there exist
compact subintervals
\[
J_1\Subset I_1,
\qquad
J_2\Subset I_2
\]
and points \(s\in J_2\), \(t\in J_1\) such that one of the two directional
kernels vanishes:
\[
K_{21}(s,t):=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t))=0,
\]
or
\[
K_{12}(t,s):=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s))=0,
\]
where
\[
F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h)).
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Because $L$ is $2$-secant to each branch, Lemma~\ref{lem:fixed-slope-intercept-sets}
shows that $m$ lies in the interior tangent-slope range of each interval.
Hence each branch has a unique interior tangent line of slope $m$, with tangent
intercept
\[
\tau_{I_i}(m)=\inf \mathcal B_{I_i}(m).
\]

At a generic turning boundary,
\[
\frac{dy/dh}{dx/dh}=\frac{N(h)}{M(h)}
\]
has one-sided blow-up, so no finite-slope tangency can occur arbitrarily close
to $h_b$. Therefore, after shrinking away a boundary neighborhood and applying
Lemma~\ref{lem:support-overlap-truncation-turning}, we may choose compact
subintervals
\[
J_1\Subset I_1,
\qquad
J_2\Subset I_2
\]
such that:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(b\in \mathcal B_{J_1}(m)\cap \mathcal B_{J_2}(m)\);
\item the unique slope-$m$ tangency point of each branch lies in the
corresponding \(J_i\).
\end{enumerate}
Hence each \(\mathcal B_{J_i}(m)\) is a compact interval whose lower endpoint
is the tangent intercept
\[
\tau_i:=\tau_{J_i}(m)=\inf \mathcal B_{J_i}(m).
\]

Now set
\[
b_*:=\inf\bigl(\mathcal B_{J_1}(m)\cap \mathcal B_{J_2}(m)\bigr).
\]
Since the intersection of two compact intervals is again a compact interval,
\[
b_*=\max(\tau_1,\tau_2).
\]
So \(b_*\) is equal to one of the two tangent intercepts, and it still lies in
the support set of the other branch.

If \(b_*=\tau_2\), then the slope-$m$ tangent line to \(\Gamma(J_2)\) meets
\(\Gamma(J_1)\). Thus there are points \(s\in J_2\), \(t\in J_1\) such that
\[
\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t))=0,
\]
i.e.
\[
K_{21}(s,t)=0.
\]

If \(b_*=\tau_1\), then the slope-$m$ tangent line to \(\Gamma(J_1)\) meets
\(\Gamma(J_2)\). Thus there are points \(t\in J_1\), \(s\in J_2\) such that
\[
\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s))=0,
\]
i.e.
\[
K_{12}(t,s)=0.
\]
This proves the lemma.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Audit: directional kernel orientation]
\label{rem:audit-directional-kernel-orientation}
Lemma~\ref{lem:pair-pair-gives-directional-kernel-zero} yields a compact
interior zero of one of the two directional tangent-separator kernels. What
remains to be shown for a one-sided turning-boundary barrier theorem is an
orientation principle forcing the relevant overlap endpoint to come from the
right branch, equivalently an argument selecting \(K_{21}\) rather than merely
one of \(K_{21},K_{12}\). The present reduction therefore moves the turning
problem from boundary-local geometry to compact directional-kernel geometry,
but does not yet identify a preferred direction.
\end{remark}

This is the clean bridge from corrected support overlap to a compact kernel zero.

\begin{remark}[Current geometric bottleneck]

\begin{remark}[Current state of the argument]
\label{rem:current-state-of-argument}
The local one-pair analysis now consists of three layers:
the cubic leading scalar $c(h)$, the quintic fixed-sector refinement
$A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h)$, and the septic determinant $\Delta_7(h)$.
These determine the normalized planar curve
\[
\Gamma(h)=\left(\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}\right),
\]
together with the derivative numerators
\[
M(h)=c(h)v_5'(h)-c'(h)v_5(h),
\qquad
N(h)=c(h)\Delta_7'(h)-c'(h)\Delta_7(h),
\]
and the projective Wronskian
\[
W(h)=
\det\!
\begin{pmatrix}
c&v_5&\Delta_7\\
c'&v_5'&\Delta_7'\\
c''&v_5''&\Delta_7''
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

On intervals where $c$, $M$, and $W$ do not vanish, the triple
$(c,v_5,\Delta_7)$ is an ECT system. Under the current direct septic
nonvanishing hypothesis, open-interval flatness $W\equiv 0$ is excluded on the
$c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0$ region by Proposition~\ref{prop:no-W-flat-interval}.
At isolated finite-order $W=0,\ M\neq 0$ boundaries,
Theorem~\ref{thm:finite-order-w-zero-local-sterility} shows that no local
pair-pair birth occurs.

Thus the remaining geometric issue is now concentrated in turning-boundary
continuation: if a finite-slope line is $2$-secant to both adjacent branches
across a generic turning boundary, then
Lemma~\ref{lem:pair-pair-gives-directional-kernel-zero} produces a compact
interior zero of one of the two directional kernels
\[
K_{21}(s,t)=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t)),
\qquad
K_{12}(t,s)=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s)).
\]
What remains audited at the geometric level is therefore the compact
directional-kernel / zero-capture problem, together with the orientation issue
isolated in Remark~\ref{rem:audit-directional-kernel-orientation}.

The remaining audited issues overall are:
first-order surjectivity, the quintic pure-value quotient detector, the full
order-$7$ fixed-sector algebra, and the turning-boundary directional-kernel
closure.
\end{remark}

I would not make these edits

I would leave these as they are in this draft:

    the parity elimination lemma and the weight-one septic reduction corollary,

    the exact identities for WW and W′W′,

    the septic K1K1​ trace-target remark,

    the “pure value slice” wording.

Those are already captured here.

latex
\begin{corollary}[No projective-Wronskian flatness on an \(M\neq 0\) region]
\label{cor:no-W-flatness-M-nonzero}
Assume that the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-driven slice of \(\Delta_7(h)\) is
generically nonzero on the quintic-generic region. Let \(J\) be a connected
open interval on which
\[
c(h)\neq 0,
\qquad
M(h)\neq 0.
\]
Then
\[
W(h)\not\equiv 0
\]
on \(J\). Equivalently,
\[
K_\Gamma(h)\not\equiv 0
\]
on \(J\).
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
Suppose \(W\equiv 0\) on \(J\). By
Proposition~\ref{prop:projective-wronskian-bridge},
\[
0=c(h)\,W(h)=M(h)N'(h)-N(h)M'(h)
\]
on \(J\). Since \(M(h)\neq 0\) on \(J\), it follows that
\[
\left(\frac{N}{M}\right)'=0,
\]
so
\[
\frac{N}{M}=A
\]
for some constant \(A\in\mathbf C\). By
Definition~\ref{def:MN-normalized-curve},
\[
\frac{\rho_2'}{\rho_1'}=\frac{N}{M}=A,
\]
hence
\[
\rho_2'=A\,\rho_1'.
\]
Integrating on the connected interval \(J\) gives
\[
\rho_2=A\,\rho_1+B
\]
for some constant \(B\in\mathbf C\). Equivalently,
\[
\Delta_7=A\,v_5+B\,c
\]
on \(J\), contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:rho2-not-affine-in-rho1}. Therefore \(W\not\equiv 0\) on
\(J\). The equivalent statement for \(K_\Gamma\) follows from
\(K_\Gamma=W/c^3\).
\end{proof}



Third, add a **short interpretive remark** right after that new corollary. This is the cleanest way to connect the λ-line back to the direct septic slice without pretending the whole total pair ((u_7,v_7)) is closed.

latex
\begin{remark}[Interpretation of the \(\lambda\)-reduction]
\label{rem:lambda-reduction-no-W-flatness}
On the direct highest-new slice one has
\[
\Delta_7^{[1]}=\lambda^{[1]}v_5
\]
by Corollary~\ref{cor:direct-q7-septic-slice}. Thus if \(W\equiv 0\) on a
connected open interval with \(c\neq 0\) and \(M\neq 0\), then the affine
dependence
\[
\Delta_7=A\,v_5+B\,c
\]
forced in the proof above implies
\[
\Delta_7^{[1]}=A\,v_5,
\]
hence
\[
\lambda^{[1]}\equiv A
\]
on that interval. In this sense, the present argument excludes \(W\)-flatness
on an \(M\neq 0\) region without requiring a separate differential equation
for \(\lambda^{[1]}\); what remains open is the contribution of the
lower/mixed septic sectors to the full total pair \((u_7,v_7)\).
\end{remark}



I would also add one **audit remark**, but only if you want the draft to make the remaining caveat explicit. Put it immediately after the λ-remark above. This is the honest boundary of what the λ-line has actually achieved.

latex
\begin{remark}[Audit: no infinite-order \(W\)-flatness]
\label{rem:audit-no-infinite-order-W-flatness}
Corollary~\ref{cor:no-W-flatness-M-nonzero} excludes \(W\equiv 0\) on any open
interval with \(c\neq 0\) and \(M\neq 0\). To conclude that every
\(W=0,\ M\neq 0\) boundary is finite-order, one still needs the regularity
input that \(W\) is real-analytic on the relevant generic region. Once that is
recorded explicitly, infinite-order flatness is ruled out as well.
\end{remark}

I’m checking the updated draft as a whole now, not just the new insertions. The main questions are whether the W=0W=0 side is now honestly reduced the way we intended, whether there are still duplicated/conditional statements hiding elsewhere, and what the single narrowest remaining theorem is.

Holistically: the architecture is now in the right place, and the important new reductions are captured.

What is now genuinely in the draft is the right split of the geometry. The W=0, M≠0W=0, M=0 side is no longer treated as one vague obstruction: open-interval flatness is excluded under the current direct septic hypothesis via the affine-dependence contradiction, and finite-order isolated W=0W=0 boundaries are locally sterile for pair-pair birth. The turning-boundary side is also sharper now: you have local support-overlap truncation and the directional-kernel bridge, so the turning problem has been reduced from “mysterious local geometry” to a compact interior kernel problem. That is a real structural improvement, and it matches the direction the other thread had converged to.

But no, not everything needed for a full proof is captured yet.

The narrowest remaining gaps now look like this.

First, the geometric closure is still not done. The current draft only gets you from an adjacent pair-pair line to a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels. It does not yet convert that into the one-sided kernel theorem the rest of the turning-boundary stack wants. In other words, the geometry has been reduced to the directional-kernel / zero-capture problem, but that problem is still open. Your own current bottleneck/state remarks now basically say that, and I think they are correct to do so.

Second, the local algebra is still not fully closed. The remaining audited list is now much smaller, but it is still real: first-order surjectivity, quintic pure-value trace annihilation or a substitute detector, the full total septic fixed-sector pair (u7,v7)(u7​,v7​), and the direct septic witness as a standalone local theorem. Those are no longer sprawling unknowns, but they are still genuine missing pieces.

Third, the endgame still overstates the proof status. The draft now honestly says in its WIP remark that the contradiction theorem is written in the pair-like branch and that the genuinely finite-core branch is not finished, but the theorem statement still says “Consequently the Riemann Hypothesis holds.” That remains stronger than the proved architecture currently warrants. The draft is coherent as a work-in-progress proof program; it is not yet internally aligned as a finished proof document.

So my honest summary is:

    as a research draft / architecture: yes, the important new picture is now captured;

    as a complete proof: no, the last serious theorem stack is still open.

The next plan of attack should now be much narrower than before.

I would stop spending time on the W=0W=0 side unless a new obstruction appears. That front is now sufficiently reduced:
finite-order W=0W=0 is locally sterile, and W≡0W≡0 on an M≠0M=0 region is excluded under the current direct-septic hypothesis. The main battle is not there anymore.

The next attack should be:

1. Symmetrize the turning-boundary kernel machinery.
Right now your bridge lemma yields a compact interior zero of either K21K21​ or K12K12​. The clean next theorem is not to force orientation immediately, but to build the compact first-zero / first-zero-implies-tangency argument for both directional kernels. That avoids needing a premature orientation theorem.

Concretely, I would prove the following pair of lemmas in parallel on compact interior subintervals J1⋐I1J1​⋐I1​, J2⋐I2J2​⋐I2​:
K21(s,t)=det⁡(F(s),F′(s),F(t)),K12(t,s)=det⁡(F(t),F′(t),F(s)).
K21​(s,t)=det(F(s),F′(s),F(t)),K12​(t,s)=det(F(t),F′(t),F(s)).

For each kernel separately:

    compact first-zero realization;

    first-zero implies tangency by the implicit function theorem;

    tangency contradicts the local support-ordering / endpoint exclusion already known near the turning boundary.

Once that is in place, the “directional kernel orientation” issue may simply disappear, because you will not care which direction the overlap endpoint selects.

That is the single best next theorem stack.

2. Only after that, close the global no-four-secant theorem through support transitivity.
Once one of the directional kernels is ruled out on every adjacent pair, the support-order argument becomes the final geometric closure. This is the stage where the current draft still says “if adjacent sign regularity is available, the global no-secant theorem follows.” That implication is no longer the main problem; the adjacent directional-kernel closure is.

3. Then return to the local algebraic audits in this order:
first the quintic pure-value trace issue, then the full total septic fixed-sector pair, then the first-order surjectivity cleanup. The reason for that order is practical: the quintic pure-value point is the smallest local theorem that affects the first residual layer directly, while the full septic pair is the largest remaining algebraic package.

So the shortest “next plan” is:

    freeze the current W=0W=0 reductions;

    prove a symmetrized compact directional-kernel exclusion theorem at generic turning boundaries;

    derive the global no-four-secant consequence from that;

    then finish the remaining local algebra.

    K21(s,t)=det⁡(F(s),F′(s),F(t)),K12(t,s)=det⁡(F(t),F′(t),F(s))

Conditional symmetrized directional-kernel exclusion theorem

Let I1<I2I1​<I2​ be adjacent ECT intervals across a generic turning boundary
M(hb)=0,M′(hb)≠0,N(hb)≠0.
M(hb​)=0,M′(hb​)=0,N(hb​)=0.

K21(s,t):=det⁡(F(s),F′(s),F(t)),s∈I2, t∈I1,

K12(t,s):=det⁡(F(t),F′(t),F(s)),t∈I1, s∈I2,
K12​(t,s):=det(F(t),F′(t),F(s)),t∈I1​, s∈I2​,

with F=(c,v5,Δ7)F=(c,v5​,Δ7​).

Assume the two directional zero-capture hypotheses:

    every zero of K21K21​ on I2×I1I2​×I1​ lies in some compact interior rectangle J2×J1⊂I2×I1J2​×J1​⊂I2​×I1​;

    every zero of K12K12​ on I1×I2I1​×I2​ lies in some compact interior rectangle J1′×J2′⊂I1×I2J1′​×J2′​⊂I1​×I2​.

Then
K21(s,t)≠0(s∈I2, t∈I1),
K21​(s,t)=0(s∈I2​, t∈I1​),

and
K12(t,s)≠0(t∈I1, s∈I2).
K12​(t,s)=0(t∈I1​, s∈I2​).

So under symmetric zero-capture, both directional kernels are excluded across a generic turning boundary.
Why this is the right theorem

Once you have this, the bridge lemma from adjacent pair-pair overlap to a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels becomes enough. You no longer need a separate orientation theorem selecting “the right branch tangent” in advance. Whatever direction the overlap endpoint chooses, that kernel is forbidden.

That is a real simplification.
Proof skeleton for K21K21​

This is basically the turning-boundary theorem you already have, but stated cleanly as conditional on zero-capture.

Assume K21K21​ has some zero. By endpoint-tangent exclusion, there is a boundary-near region where K21≠0K21​=0. By the K21K21​ zero-capture hypothesis, all zeros lie in a compact interior rectangle J2×J1J2​×J1​. Then compact first-zero reduction gives a zero
(s∗,t∗)∈J2×J1
(s∗​,t∗​)∈J2​×J1​

whose ss-coordinate is boundary-nearest in the x=v5/cx=v5​/c ordering. The existing first-zero-implies-tangency step then yields
K21(s∗,t∗)=0,∂tK21(s∗,t∗)=0.
K21​(s∗​,t∗​)=0,∂t​K21​(s∗​,t∗​)=0.

So the tangent line at Γ(s∗)Γ(s∗​) is also tangent at Γ(t∗)Γ(t∗​). That gives a common tangent slope across the adjacent branches.

Now there are only two possibilities.

If both tangencies are interior, this contradicts adjacent slope-range separation at a generic turning boundary. If one tangency is at the boundary endpoint slope, this contradicts endpoint-tangent exclusion. Therefore no such zero exists.

That is exactly the current one-sided argument, just honestly packaged as conditional. The draft/convo already has this structure and already identifies zero-capture as the live missing theorem.
Mirrored proof for K12K12​

This is the genuinely new part, but it is just the reflected version.

Assume K12K12​ has a zero. By the same endpoint-tangent exclusion logic, there is a boundary-near region where K12≠0K12​=0. By the K12K12​ zero-capture hypothesis, all zeros lie in a compact interior rectangle J1′×J2′J1′​×J2′​. Choose a first zero
(t∗,s∗)∈J1′×J2′
(t∗​,s∗​)∈J1′​×J2′​

whose tt-coordinate is boundary-nearest in the x=v5/cx=v5​/c ordering on the left branch.

Then the mirrored first-zero lemma should say:
K12(t∗,s∗)=0⟹∂sK12(t∗,s∗)=0
K12​(t∗​,s∗​)=0⟹∂s​K12​(t∗​,s∗​)=0

at this first zero.

This is the exact left-right analogue of the existing K21K21​ first-zero-implies-tangency step. The proof is the same implicit-function-theorem argument: if ∂sK12≠0∂s​K12​=0, then locally the zero set is a graph s=s(t)s=s(t), which lets you continue the zero to a boundary-nearer tt, contradicting the choice of first zero.

Now
∂sK12(t,s)=det⁡(F(t),F′(t),F′(s)),
∂s​K12​(t,s)=det(F(t),F′(t),F′(s)),

so
K12(t∗,s∗)=0,∂sK12(t∗,s∗)=0
K12​(t∗​,s∗​)=0,∂s​K12​(t∗​,s∗​)=0

means the tangent line at Γ(t∗)Γ(t∗​) is also tangent at Γ(s∗)Γ(s∗​). Again this gives a common tangent slope across adjacent branches, and the same two contradictions apply:

    interior common slope is forbidden by adjacent slope-range separation;

    endpoint boundary-slope tangency is forbidden by endpoint-tangent exclusion.

So K12K12​ has no zero either.
The only new lemma you actually need

So the next surgical lemma is not a huge new theorem. It is just the mirrored first-zero step:

    Mirrored first-zero-implies-tangency.
    On a compact interior rectangle J1×J2⊂I1×I2J1​×J2​⊂I1​×I2​, if (t∗,s∗)(t∗​,s∗​) is a first zero of K12(t,s)K12​(t,s) in the boundary-nearest tt-ordering, then
    ∂sK12(t∗,s∗)=0.
    ∂s​K12​(t∗​,s∗​)=0. 

Once you write that down, the rest is literally the same proof as for K21K21​.
Why I think this is the right frontier

Because it converts the turning-boundary geometry into a purely symmetric compact-kernel problem. And that meshes perfectly with the bridge lemma you already have: adjacent pair-pair overlap only gives you a compact zero of one of the directional kernels, not a preferred one. So the natural way forward is not to prove an orientation theorem yet, but to kill both directions.

That is stronger and cleaner.
What this gives immediately afterward

Once the symmetric directional-kernel exclusion theorem is in place, the adjacent pair-pair exclusion theorem becomes immediate:

    pair-pair line across adjacent turning-boundary branches

    ⇒⇒ compact zero of one of K21,K12K21​,K12​

    ⇒⇒ contradiction.

Then the global no-four-secant consequence follows by the same support transitivity the draft already treats as the last step after adjacent sign regularity.

That is the smallest missing piece in the symmetrized closure.

1. The mirrored first-zero lemma

Define
K12(t,s):=det⁡(F(t),F′(t),F(s)),t∈I1, s∈I2,

(t∗,s∗)∈J1×J2
(t∗​,s∗​)∈J1​×J2​

Proof

s=s(t)
s=s(t)

through (t∗,s∗)(t∗​,s∗​) on some open interval t∈(t∗−ε,t∗+ε)t∈(t∗​−ε,t∗​+ε). Because J1⋐I1J1​⋐I1​ is compact interior and t∗t∗​ is chosen as the first zero in the tt-ordering, we may arrange J1J1​ so that t∗t∗​ is not trapped at the left endpoint of the compact box: there is room inside J1J1​ for t<t∗t<t∗​ arbitrarily close to t∗t∗​.

But then for t<t∗t<t∗​ sufficiently close, the point (t,s(t))(t,s(t)) is also a zero of K12K12​, contradicting the minimality of t∗t∗​.

Hence
∂sK12(t∗,s∗)=0.
∂s​K12​(t∗​,s∗​)=0.

K12(t,s)=det⁡(F(t),F′(t),F(s)),∂sK12(t,s)=det⁡(F(t),F′(t),F′(s)).
K12​(t,s)=det(F(t),F′(t),F(s)),∂s​K12​(t,s)=det(F(t),F′(t),F′(s)).

So at (t∗,s∗)(t∗​,s∗​),
K12(t∗,s∗)=0,∂sK12(t∗,s∗)=0
K12​(t∗​,s∗​)=0,∂s​K12​(t∗​,s∗​)=0

    and the tangent direction at s∗s∗​ is along that same line.

So the tangent line at Γ(t∗)Γ(t∗​) is also tangent at Γ(s∗)Γ(s∗​). This is exactly the left-right mirror of the existing K21K21​ first-zero lemma. Compare the current one-sided first-zero/tangency mechanism already in the draft/convo.

Now assume I1<I2I1​<I2​ are adjacent ECT intervals across a generic turning boundary
M(hb)=0,M′(hb)≠0,N(hb)≠0.

Then both kernels are zero-free:
K21(s,t)≠0(s∈I2, t∈I1),
K21​(s,t)=0(s∈I2​, t∈I1​),

and
K12(t,s)≠0(t∈I1, s∈I2).
K12​(t,s)=0(t∈I1​, s∈I2​).
Proof for K21K21​

Proof for K12K12​

The first is in the mirrored lemma proof. The sentence

That avoids talking about the left edge of the compact box. The contradiction is with minimality in the **ambient domain**, not with the box geometry. The compact box is only there to guarantee the selected zero is interior enough for the implicit-function continuation.

[
\phi_m(h)=\frac{\Delta_7(h)-m v_5(h)}{c(h)}

for every finite slope (m), and (x=v_5/c) has a strict local extremum, so both finite-slope and vertical directions are locally ordered. That is the clean local reason a tangent from one side cannot meet the other branch arbitrarily near the turning boundary, in either orientation. I would say that explicitly rather than just “by the same endpoint-tangent exclusion logic.”

    zero-capture: every zero of K12K12​ on I1×I2I1​×I2​ lies in some compact interior rectangle
    J1×J2⋐I1×I2.
    J1​×J2​⋐I1​×I2​.

Choose a zero
(t∗,s∗)
(t∗​,s∗​)

with t∗t∗​ minimal in the x=v5/cx=v5​/c ordering among all zeros of K12K12​ on I1×I2I1​×I2​.

s=s(t)
s=s(t)

K12​(t,s(t))=0.

\begin{lemma}[Boundary-near exclusion for both directional kernels at a generic turning boundary]
\label{lem:boundary-near-directional-kernel-exclusion}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Define
\[
K_{21}(s,t):=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t)),
\qquad
K_{12}(t,s):=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s)),
\]
with
\[
F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h)).
\]
Then there is a neighborhood of the boundary pair on which both directional
kernels are nonzero:
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0,
\qquad
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\]
for \(s\in I_2\), \(t\in I_1\) sufficiently close to \(h_b\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Write
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
y(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}.
\]
Since
\[
y'(h)=\frac{N(h)}{c(h)^2},
\]
the hypothesis \(N(h_b)\neq 0\) implies that \(y\) is a valid local
coordinate near \(h_b\). Thus the normalized planar curve may be written
locally as a graph
\[
x=\chi(y).
\]
At \(h_b\),
\[
\frac{dx}{dy}=\frac{M(h)}{N(h)},
\]
so
\[
\left.\frac{dx}{dy}\right|_{h_b}=0.
\]
Differentiating once more,
\[
\frac{d^2x}{dy^2}
=
\frac{c(h)^2}{N(h)}\cdot\frac{M'(h)N(h)-M(h)N'(h)}{N(h)^2},
\]
and since \(M(h_b)=0\),
\[
\left.\frac{d^2x}{dy^2}\right|_{h_b}
=
\frac{c(h_b)^2\,M'(h_b)}{N(h_b)^2}\neq 0.
\]
Hence, after shrinking to a small neighborhood of \(h_b\), the graph
\(x=\chi(y)\) is strictly convex or strictly concave.

For such a graph, the tangent line at any point meets the graph only at its
point of tangency. Therefore no tangent line from one side can meet the
opposite side inside a sufficiently small neighborhood of the boundary.
Equivalently, both directional tangent-separator kernels are nonzero near the
boundary pair.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Mirrored first adjacent zero implies tangency]
\label{lem:mirrored-first-adjacent-zero-implies-tangency}
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\), \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be compact interior subintervals
across a generic turning boundary, and define
\[
K_{12}(t,s):=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s)),
\qquad
t\in I_1,\ s\in I_2.
\]
Assume every zero of \(K_{12}\) on \(I_1\times I_2\) lies in
\(J_1\times J_2\). Let \((t_*,s_*)\in J_1\times J_2\) be a zero whose
\(t\)-coordinate is minimal in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all zeros of
\(K_{12}\) on \(I_1\times I_2\). Then
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Because \(M\neq 0\) on the open interval \(I_1\), the function
\[
x(h)=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)}
\]
is a valid local coordinate on \(I_1\). Since every zero of \(K_{12}\) lies in
the compact interior rectangle \(J_1\times J_2\), the chosen point
\((t_*,s_*)\) is interior in the ambient domain \(I_1\times I_2\).

Suppose
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)\neq 0.
\]
Then by the implicit function theorem there is a \(C^1\) local zero curve
\[
s=s(t)
\]
through \((t_*,s_*)\), defined for \(t\) near \(t_*\), such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s(t))=0.
\]
Because \(t_*\) is interior in \(I_1\), there are nearby points with smaller
\(x=v_5/c\) ordering still in \(I_1\). For such \(t\), the point
\((t,s(t))\) is also a zero of \(K_{12}\), contradicting the minimality of
\(t_*\). Therefore
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Conditional symmetric directional-kernel exclusion across turning boundaries]
\label{thm:conditional-symmetric-directional-kernel-exclusion}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the zero-capture hypothesis required in
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity} for the kernel
\(K_{21}\) on \(I_2\times I_1\);
\item the symmetric zero-capture hypothesis for \(K_{12}\), namely that every
zero of
\[
K_{12}(t,s)=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s))
\]
on \(I_1\times I_2\) lies in some compact interior rectangle
\(J_1\times J_2\Subset I_1\times I_2\).
\end{enumerate}
Then both directional kernels are zero-free:
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad (s\in I_2,\ t\in I_1),
\]
and
\[
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\qquad (t\in I_1,\ s\in I_2).
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The first assertion is exactly
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}.

For the second assertion, boundary-near exclusion for \(K_{12}\) is provided by
Lemma~\ref{lem:boundary-near-directional-kernel-exclusion}. Assume for
contradiction that \(K_{12}\) has a zero. By the symmetric zero-capture
hypothesis, every zero lies in a compact interior rectangle \(J_1\times J_2\).
Choose a zero \((t_*,s_*)\in J_1\times J_2\) whose \(t\)-coordinate is minimal
in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all zeros of \(K_{12}\) on \(I_1\times I_2\).

By Lemma~\ref{lem:mirrored-first-adjacent-zero-implies-tangency},
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
Together with
\[
K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0,
\]
this implies that the tangent line at \(\Gamma(t_*)\) is also tangent at
\(\Gamma(s_*)\). Hence the two adjacent intervals share a common interior
tangent slope, contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}. Therefore
\(K_{12}\) has no zero.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Status of the directional-kernel reduction]
\label{rem:status-directional-kernel-reduction}
Lemma~\ref{lem:pair-pair-gives-directional-kernel-zero} reduces an adjacent
pair-pair line across a generic turning boundary to a compact interior zero of
one of the two directional tangent-separator kernels. Theorem~\ref{thm:conditional-symmetric-directional-kernel-exclusion}
shows that the remaining turning-boundary issue is no longer orientation
selection between \(K_{21}\) and \(K_{12}\), but the symmetric zero-capture
problem for these two kernels.
\end{remark}

FYI here's the latest paper draft

I’m checking the latest draft against the symmetric zero-capture frontier specifically: whether there is now enough structure to try to close it outright, or whether one missing ingredient still makes that jump too aggressive. Then I’ll tell you bluntly whether it looks closable now or whether it should be split into a smaller theorem first.

I would not assume we can just close symmetric zero-capture from the current ingredients.

My honest read is: probably not as a clean theorem of the form “all zeros of both directional kernels lie in a compact interior box”. The current draft/convo still treats zero-capture as the live turning-boundary theorem, and nothing in the latest push obviously removes the core difficulty that a zero sequence could escape toward a non-common endpoint of I1I1​ or I2I2​, not just toward the shared boundary. That is exactly why the current turning-boundary theorem was made conditional in the first place.

What is now in place is stronger local control:

    finite-order W=0, M≠0W=0, M=0 boundaries are locally sterile for pair-pair birth;

    generic turning boundaries have local support ordering / boundary-near exclusion;

    pair-pair overlap across a generic turning boundary yields a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels;

    the mirrored first-zero lemma and the symmetric conditional theorem reduce the remaining issue to symmetric zero-capture for K21K21​ and K12K12​.

So the aggressive thing to do now is not try to prove full symmetric zero-capture in one jump. I think the better move is to attack a theorem that is both weaker and more closely tailored to the global obstruction.

The right replacement target is:

    pair-pair-generated zero-capture, not full zero-capture.

In other words:

If a finite-slope line is 22-secant to both adjacent branches across a generic turning boundary, then the directional-kernel zero produced by the compact support-overlap endpoint argument is already realized on a compact interior pair, and the first-zero/tangency contradiction can be run inside that compact overlap family, without needing a theorem about all other zeros of the kernel on the whole open rectangle.

That avoids the hardest part of zero-capture entirely: you no longer need to control arbitrary stray zeros near far endpoints that may be irrelevant to the pair-pair obstruction.

So my blunt answer is:

    Can we aggressively attack symmetric zero-capture? Yes.

    Do I think we can cleanly close the full theorem right now? No, not from the current ingredients alone.

    Do I think we can likely bypass full zero-capture with a more targeted compact first-contact theorem tied to support overlap? Yes, and that is where I would push next.

The theorem I would try to prove next is this:

    Let I1<I2I1​<I2​ be adjacent across a generic turning boundary. Suppose a finite-slope line is 22-secant to both branches. Then, after the support-overlap truncation already in the draft, there exists a compact interior overlap family for which the overlap endpoint produces a directional kernel zero that is first in the relevant branch-ordering within that compact family, and hence yields the tangency contradiction.

If that works, you do not need global zero-capture for all kernel zeros. You only need a compact first-contact theorem for zeros arising from actual support overlap.

That is a much more plausible frontier than “prove every zero of K21K21​ and K12K12​ on the whole adjacent rectangle is trapped in a compact interior box.”

So my recommendation is: do not try to close full symmetric zero-capture as the next move. Attack the narrower compact-overlap/first-contact theorem instead. It is much closer to the actual no-four-secant obstruction, and it uses the machinery you now already have.

Do I think we can likely bypass full zero-capture with a more targeted compact first-contact theorem tied to support overlap? Yes, and that is where I would push next. --> lets do it

Yes — I think there is a real way to bypass full symmetric zero-capture.

The key is to replace “capture all zeros of the kernel on the whole open rectangle” with a much weaker and more relevant statement:

    for a fixed compact opposite branch J1⋐I1J1​⋐I1​, tangencies from the adjacent branch I2I2​ that are sufficiently close to the shared turning boundary cannot hit J1J1​ at all.

That gives a strip exclusion lemma, and once you have that, the compact first-contact argument works on the actual compact overlap family coming from pair-pair, with no need to capture arbitrary stray zeros elsewhere.

So the attack I think works is this.
1. Prove a strip exclusion lemma

Let I1<I2I1​<I2​ be adjacent across a generic turning boundary hbhb​, and work in the affine chart
x(h)=v5(h)c(h),y(h)=Δ7(h)c(h).
x(h)=c(h)v5​(h)​,y(h)=c(h)Δ7​(h)​.

Near hbhb​, the draft already gives
x′(hb)=0,x′′(hb)≠0,y′(hb)≠0,
x′(hb​)=0,x′′(hb​)=0,y′(hb​)=0,

so after using yy as local parameter, the normalized curve is locally
x=χ(y)
x=χ(y)

with χ′′(0)≠0χ′′(0)=0. Thus xx has a strict local extremum at the boundary, and the tangent slope
m(y)=dydx=1χ′(y)
m(y)=dxdy​=χ′(y)1​

blows up one-sidedly as y→0±y→0±.

Now let J1⋐I1J1​⋐I1​ be any compact subinterval away from the shared boundary.
Lemma (strip exclusion for K21K21​)

There exists a one-sided neighborhood U2⊂I2U2​⊂I2​ of the shared boundary such that
K21(s,t)=det⁡(F(s),F′(s),F(t))≠0(s∈U2, t∈J1).
K21​(s,t)=det(F(s),F′(s),F(t))=0(s∈U2​, t∈J1​).
Proof

Write the two branches in the local yy-coordinate. Since J1J1​ is compact away from the boundary, there is a fixed δ>0δ>0 such that for all t∈J1t∈J1​,
∣x(t)−x(hb)∣≥2δ.
∣x(t)−x(hb​)∣≥2δ.

For s∈I2s∈I2​ sufficiently near hbhb​,
∣x(s)−x(hb)∣≤δ,
∣x(s)−x(hb​)∣≤δ,

so
∣x(t)−x(s)∣≥δ(t∈J1).
∣x(t)−x(s)∣≥δ(t∈J1​).

The tangent line at ss has slope m(s)m(s), and the condition that it passes through tt is
y(t)−y(s)=m(s)(x(t)−x(s)).
y(t)−y(s)=m(s)(x(t)−x(s)).

But as s→hbs→hb​ on the I2I2​-side, m(s)→±∞m(s)→±∞, while
y(t)−y(s)
y(t)−y(s)

remains uniformly bounded for t∈J1t∈J1​, and
x(t)−x(s)
x(t)−x(s)

stays bounded away from 00. Therefore the right-hand side has arbitrarily large magnitude and fixed sign, while the left-hand side stays bounded. So equality is impossible for ss sufficiently close to hbhb​.

Hence
K21(s,t)≠0
K21​(s,t)=0

for all s∈U2, t∈J1s∈U2​, t∈J1​.

The same argument gives the mirror statement:
Lemma (strip exclusion for K12K12​)

For every compact J2⋐I2J2​⋐I2​, there exists a one-sided neighborhood U1⊂I1U1​⊂I1​ of the boundary such that
K12(t,s)=det⁡(F(t),F′(t),F(s))≠0(t∈U1, s∈J2).
K12​(t,s)=det(F(t),F′(t),F(s))=0(t∈U1​, s∈J2​).

This is the crucial replacement for full zero-capture.
2. Use strip exclusion to get a compact first-contact theorem

Now suppose the bridge lemma has already produced a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels, say K21K21​, on a compact pair
J2⋐I2,J1⋐I1.
J2​⋐I2​,J1​⋐I1​.

Enlarge J2J2​ slightly toward the shared boundary so that its boundary-facing collar lies in the strip U2U2​ from the previous lemma, while still remaining compact and interior.

Define the compact zero set
Z21:={(s,t)∈J2×J1: K21(s,t)=0}.
Z21​:={(s,t)∈J2​×J1​: K21​(s,t)=0}.

If Z21≠∅Z21​=∅, let
A21:={s∈J2:∃ t∈J1 with K21(s,t)=0}.
A21​:={s∈J2​:∃t∈J1​ with K21​(s,t)=0}.

Since Z21Z21​ is compact, A21A21​ is compact. Since the boundary-facing collar of J2J2​ is zero-free against all t∈J1t∈J1​, the set A21A21​ is bounded away from that collar. So A21A21​ has a boundary-nearest element s∗s∗​ that is still an interior point of J2J2​. Pick t∗∈J1t∗​∈J1​ with
K21(s∗,t∗)=0.
K21​(s∗​,t∗​)=0.

Now argue exactly as in the old first-zero lemma, but only inside this compact family:

if
∂tK21(s∗,t∗)≠0,
∂t​K21​(s∗​,t∗​)=0,

then the implicit function theorem gives a local zero curve
t=t(s)
t=t(s)

through (s∗,t∗)(s∗​,t∗​), so for ss slightly boundary-nearer than s∗s∗​, still inside J2J2​, we get another zero (s,t(s))∈Z21(s,t(s))∈Z21​, contradicting the minimality of s∗s∗​ in A21A21​.

Therefore
∂tK21(s∗,t∗)=0.
∂t​K21​(s∗​,t∗​)=0.

Together with
K21(s∗,t∗)=0,
K21​(s∗​,t∗​)=0,

this means the tangent line at Γ(s∗)Γ(s∗​) is also tangent at Γ(t∗)Γ(t∗​).

So from a compact directional-kernel zero we get a common tangent line across the adjacent branches, without any global zero-capture theorem.

The same argument mirrors for K12K12​.
3. This gives the actual bypass theorem

So the theorem to prove next is:
Theorem (compact first-contact exclusion for pair-pair-generated directional zeros)

Let I1<I2I1​<I2​ be adjacent across a generic turning boundary. Suppose a finite-slope line is 22-secant to both branches. Then the support-overlap truncation and overlap-endpoint lemma produce a compact interior zero of one of K21K21​ or K12K12​. For either direction, the strip exclusion lemma and compact first-contact argument force a common tangent line across the two adjacent branches. This contradicts adjacent interior slope-range separation (or endpoint tangent exclusion in the endpoint case). Therefore no such adjacent pair-pair line exists.

That is the payoff. It gives you adjacent pair-pair exclusion across generic turning boundaries without full symmetric zero-capture.
4. Why this is better than full zero-capture

Because it only uses the zeros that actually arise from a genuine support-overlap / pair-pair family. It does not require any theorem about all other possible kernel zeros on the whole open rectangle I2×I1I2​×I1​ or I1×I2I1​×I2​.

So the remaining burden becomes:

    prove the strip exclusion lemma carefully,

    package the compact first-contact theorem for both directions,

    then replace the current “conditional symmetric directional-kernel exclusion” with this more targeted unconditional adjacent pair-pair exclusion theorem.

That is much more plausible than full symmetric zero-capture.
5. What I would do next

I would now try to freeze exactly these three statements in paper notation:

\begin{lemma}[Strip exclusion for \(K_{21}\) against a compact opposite branch]
\label{lem:strip-exclusion-k21}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\) be a compact subinterval. Then there exists a one-sided
neighborhood \(U_2\subset I_2\) of the shared boundary point \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t):=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t))\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in U_2,\ t\in J_1),
\]
where \(F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h))\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Work in the affine chart
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
y(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}.
\]
At a generic turning boundary one has
\[
x'(h_b)=0,\qquad x''(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad y'(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Hence \(y\) is a valid local coordinate and the normalized curve may be written
near \(h_b\) as a graph
\[
x=\chi(y)
\]
with
\[
\chi'(y_b)=0,\qquad \chi''(y_b)\neq 0.
\]
Thus \(x\) has a strict local extremum at the boundary, while the tangent slope
\[
m(h)=\frac{dy/dh}{dx/dh}=\frac{N(h)}{M(h)}
\]
blows up one-sidedly as \(h\to h_b\) from either adjacent interval.

Because \(J_1\Subset I_1\) is compact away from \(h_b\), there exists
\(\delta>0\) such that
\[
|x(t)-x(h_b)|\ge 2\delta
\qquad
(t\in J_1).
\]
After shrinking to a small one-sided neighborhood \(U_2\subset I_2\) of \(h_b\),
we may also assume
\[
|x(s)-x(h_b)|\le \delta
\qquad
(s\in U_2).
\]
Hence
\[
|x(t)-x(s)|\ge \delta
\qquad
(s\in U_2,\ t\in J_1).
\]

Fix a preliminary compact neighborhood \(U_2^0\Subset I_2\) of \(h_b\) and set
\[
B:=\sup\{|y(t)-y(s)|:\ t\in J_1,\ s\in U_2^0\}<\infty.
\]
Since \(m(s)\to \pm\infty\) as \(s\to h_b\) in \(I_2\), we may shrink further to
\(U_2\subset U_2^0\) so that
\[
|m(s)|>\frac{2B}{\delta}
\qquad
(s\in U_2).
\]

If \(K_{21}(s,t)=0\), then the tangent line at \(\Gamma(s)\) passes through
\(\Gamma(t)\), equivalently
\[
y(t)-y(s)=m(s)\bigl(x(t)-x(s)\bigr).
\]
Taking absolute values gives
\[
B\ge |y(t)-y(s)|=|m(s)|\,|x(t)-x(s)|>\frac{2B}{\delta}\cdot \delta=2B,
\]
a contradiction. Therefore \(K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0\) on \(U_2\times J_1\).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Strip exclusion for \(K_{12}\) against a compact opposite branch]
\label{lem:strip-exclusion-k12}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Let \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be a compact subinterval. Then there exists a one-sided
neighborhood \(U_1\subset I_1\) of the shared boundary point \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s):=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s))\neq 0
\qquad
(t\in U_1,\ s\in J_2),
\]
where \(F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h))\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
This is the left-right mirror of
Lemma~\ref{lem:strip-exclusion-k21}, exchanging the roles of \(I_1\) and \(I_2\).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Compact first-contact for \(K_{21}\)]
\label{lem:compact-first-contact-k21}
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\), \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be compact subintervals across a
generic turning boundary. Assume there is a relatively open collar
\(V_2\subset J_2\) at the boundary-facing end of \(J_2\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in V_2,\ t\in J_1).
\]
Assume also that the zero set of \(K_{21}\) in \(J_2\times J_1\) is nonempty.
Then there exists a zero \((s_*,t_*)\in J_2\times J_1\) such that:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(s_*\) is boundary-nearest in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all
\(s\in J_2\) for which \(K_{21}(s,t)=0\) for some \(t\in J_1\);
\item
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Define
\[
A_{21}:=\{\,s\in J_2:\ \exists\, t\in J_1 \text{ with } K_{21}(s,t)=0\,\}.
\]
Since the zero set of \(K_{21}\) in \(J_2\times J_1\) is compact and nonempty,
the set \(A_{21}\) is compact and nonempty. By hypothesis,
\[
A_{21}\cap V_2=\varnothing.
\]
Hence \(A_{21}\) has a boundary-nearest element \(s_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\)
ordering on \(J_2\). Choose \(t_*\in J_1\) with
\[
K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]

Suppose
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)\neq 0.
\]
Then by the implicit function theorem there is a \(C^1\) local zero curve
\[
t=t(s)
\]
through \((s_*,t_*)\), defined for \(s\) near \(s_*\), such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t(s))=0.
\]
Because \(s_*\notin V_2\) and \(V_2\) is an open collar at the boundary-facing
end of \(J_2\), there exist nearby points \(s\in J_2\) that are boundary-nearer
than \(s_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering. For such \(s\), the point
\((s,t(s))\) is also a zero of \(K_{21}\), contradicting the minimality of
\(s_*\). Therefore
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Compact first-contact for \(K_{12}\)]
\label{lem:compact-first-contact-k12}
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\), \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be compact subintervals across a
generic turning boundary. Assume there is a relatively open collar
\(V_1\subset J_1\) at the boundary-facing end of \(J_1\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\qquad
(t\in V_1,\ s\in J_2).
\]
Assume also that the zero set of \(K_{12}\) in \(J_1\times J_2\) is nonempty.
Then there exists a zero \((t_*,s_*)\in J_1\times J_2\) such that:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(t_*\) is boundary-nearest in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all
\(t\in J_1\) for which \(K_{12}(t,s)=0\) for some \(s\in J_2\);
\item
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Define
\[
A_{12}:=\{\,t\in J_1:\ \exists\, s\in J_2 \text{ with } K_{12}(t,s)=0\,\}.
\]
Since the zero set of \(K_{12}\) in \(J_1\times J_2\) is compact and nonempty,
the set \(A_{12}\) is compact and nonempty. By hypothesis,
\[
A_{12}\cap V_1=\varnothing.
\]
Hence \(A_{12}\) has a boundary-nearest element \(t_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\)
ordering on \(J_1\). Choose \(s_*\in J_2\) with
\[
K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]

Suppose
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)\neq 0.
\]
Then by the implicit function theorem there is a \(C^1\) local zero curve
\[
s=s(t)
\]
through \((t_*,s_*)\), defined for \(t\) near \(t_*\), such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s(t))=0.
\]
Because \(t_*\notin V_1\) and \(V_1\) is an open collar at the boundary-facing
end of \(J_1\), there exist nearby points \(t\in J_1\) that are boundary-nearer
than \(t_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering. For such \(t\), the point
\((t,s(t))\) is also a zero of \(K_{12}\), contradicting the minimality of
\(t_*\). Therefore
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Adjacent pair-pair exclusion across a generic turning boundary]
\label{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Then no finite-slope affine line in the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c}
\]
is \(2\)-secant to both \(\Gamma(I_1)\) and \(\Gamma(I_2)\).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Assume for contradiction that a finite-slope line
\[
L:\ y=mx+b
\]
is \(2\)-secant to both adjacent branches. By
Lemma~\ref{lem:pair-pair-gives-directional-kernel-zero}, after removing a
small boundary neighborhood there exist compact subintervals
\[
J_1\Subset I_1,\qquad J_2\Subset I_2
\]
and a zero of one of the two directional kernels.

First suppose there exist \(s_0\in J_2\), \(t_0\in J_1\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s_0,t_0)=0.
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:strip-exclusion-k21}, there exists a one-sided neighborhood
\(U_2\subset I_2\) of \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in U_2,\ t\in J_1).
\]
Choose a compact interval \(\widetilde J_2\Subset I_2\) containing \(s_0\) and
whose boundary-facing collar \(V_2\) lies in \(U_2\). Then the zero set of
\(K_{21}\) in \(\widetilde J_2\times J_1\) is nonempty, and
Lemma~\ref{lem:compact-first-contact-k21} yields a zero
\[
(s_*,t_*)\in \widetilde J_2\times J_1
\]
such that
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]
Together with \(K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0\), this means that the tangent line at
\(\Gamma(s_*)\) is also tangent at \(\Gamma(t_*)\). Since
\(s_*\in I_2\) and \(t_*\in I_1\) are interior points, this gives a common
interior tangent slope across the adjacent branches, contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}.

Now suppose instead there exist \(t_0\in J_1\), \(s_0\in J_2\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t_0,s_0)=0.
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:strip-exclusion-k12}, there exists a one-sided neighborhood
\(U_1\subset I_1\) of \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\qquad
(t\in U_1,\ s\in J_2).
\]
Choose a compact interval \(\widetilde J_1\Subset I_1\) containing \(t_0\) and
whose boundary-facing collar \(V_1\) lies in \(U_1\). Then the zero set of
\(K_{12}\) in \(\widetilde J_1\times J_2\) is nonempty, and
Lemma~\ref{lem:compact-first-contact-k12} yields a zero
\[
(t_*,s_*)\in \widetilde J_1\times J_2
\]
such that
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
Together with \(K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0\), this means that the tangent line at
\(\Gamma(t_*)\) is also tangent at \(\Gamma(s_*)\). Again this gives a common
interior tangent slope across the adjacent branches, contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}.

Both cases are impossible. Therefore no finite-slope line is \(2\)-secant to
both adjacent branches across a generic turning boundary.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Status of the directional-kernel reduction]
\label{rem:audit-directional-kernel-orientation}
Lemma~\ref{lem:pair-pair-gives-directional-kernel-zero} reduces an adjacent
pair-pair line across a generic turning boundary to a compact interior zero of
one of the two directional tangent-separator kernels. Theorems
\ref{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning} and
\ref{thm:conditional-symmetric-directional-kernel-exclusion} show two
different closures of that reduction:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an unconditional closure for actual adjacent pair-pair lines, via strip
exclusion and compact first-contact;
\item a stronger but still conditional closure for full zero-freeness of both
directional kernels, via symmetric zero-capture.
\end{enumerate}

Thus orientation selection between \(K_{21}\) and \(K_{12}\) is no longer the
main issue. What remains open only for the stronger tangent-separator /
support-ordering route is the symmetric zero-capture problem for these two
kernels on the full adjacent rectangle.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[What the unconditional adjacent pair-pair theorem does and does not yet replace]
\label{rem:status-adjacent-pair-pair-turning}
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning} gives an unconditional
exclusion of finite-slope pair-pair lines across a \emph{single} generic
turning-boundary adjacent pair. This is stronger than the older local
directional-kernel reduction in that it bypasses full symmetric zero-capture.

However, this theorem does not yet by itself replace the later partial global
no-secant theorem. To do that, one would still need a separate propagation
result showing that exclusion of pair-pair lines for all adjacent pairs implies
exclusion for all non-adjacent pairs as well. The current draft does not yet
contain that propagation theorem.
\end{remark}

This is the key honesty patch.

I can draft the theorem you would need, but I do not think we can honestly mark it proved from the current ingredients yet.

The exact propagation theorem that would let you replace thm:partial-global-no-secant by the new route is this:

\begin{theorem}[Propagation to an adjacent turning pair]
\label{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair}
Let
\[
I_1<I_2<\cdots<I_N
\]
be the ECT intervals on a connected generic component of the normalized curve,
and assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item every internal boundary between adjacent intervals is either
a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0,
\]
or a finite-order boundary of type
\[
W(h_b)=0,\qquad M(h_b)\neq 0;
\]

\item on every connected open subinterval with \(c\neq 0\) and \(M\neq 0\),
one has \(W\not\equiv 0\);

\item every finite-order boundary of type \(W=0,\ M\neq 0\) is locally sterile
for pair-pair birth in the sense of
Theorem~\ref{thm:finite-order-w-zero-local-sterility};
\end{enumerate}

Fix a finite-slope affine line
\[
L_{m,b}:\ y=mx+b.
\]
If \(L_{m,b}\) is \(2\)-secant to \(\Gamma(I_a)\) and to \(\Gamma(I_b)\) for
some \(a<b\), then there exists an index \(j\) with
\[
a\le j<b
\]
such that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(I_j<I_{j+1}\) meet at a generic turning boundary; and
\item \(L_{m,b}\) is \(2\)-secant to both \(\Gamma(I_j)\) and \(\Gamma(I_{j+1})\).
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}[Global no-four-secant from adjacent turning exclusion]
\label{cor:global-no-four-secant-from-adjacent-turning}
Assume the hypotheses of
Theorem~\ref{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair}, and assume in addition
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning}. Then no finite-slope
affine line in the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c}
\]
is \(2\)-secant to two distinct ECT intervals.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
If such a line existed, Theorem~\ref{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair}
would produce an adjacent pair across a generic turning boundary to which the
line is \(2\)-secant on both sides, contradicting
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning}.
\end{proof}

That is the right theorem if your goal is to replace the old thm:partial-global-no-secant route with the new turning-boundary route.

But I want to be clear: the hard content is not the corollary. It is the propagation theorem itself. And inside that theorem, there is one genuinely nontrivial sublemma.

The sublemma you need is this:

\begin{lemma}[Turning birth forces adjacent persistence]
\label{lem:turning-birth-forces-adjacent-persistence}
Under the hypotheses of
Theorem~\ref{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair},
let \(L_{m,b}\) be a finite-slope line and let
\[
\Sigma_{m,b}:=\{\,j:\ L_{m,b}\text{ is \(2\)-secant to }\Gamma(I_j)\,\}.
\]
Suppose \(j\) is the leftmost index in \(\Sigma_{m,b}\), and suppose the
boundary between \(I_j\) and \(I_{j+1}\) is generic turning. Then
\[
j+1\in \Sigma_{m,b}.
\]
Equivalently, once \(2\)-secancy is present at the first interval of a minimal
bad block, it persists across the first internal generic turning boundary.
\end{lemma}

That lemma is the real hinge. If you had it, the propagation theorem would be very believable:

    choose a pair-pair line hitting two intervals with minimal span;

    let aa be the leftmost and bb the rightmost 22-secant intervals;

    finite-order W=0W=0 local sterility and no WW-flat intervals tell you that the change from “not 22-secant” to “22-secant” cannot be born at a W=0, M≠0W=0, M=0 boundary;

    so the first birth must occur at a generic turning boundary;

    the lemma above then forces persistence into the next interval, giving an adjacent turning pair with 2+22+2 secancy.

That is exactly what would let the new adjacent theorem replace the old partial global theorem.

So my honest recommendation is:

    do not insert the propagation theorem as proved yet;

    insert it only as a target theorem / roadmap theorem if you want;

    the actual next math attack should be on
    Lemma \reflem:turning−birth−forces−adjacent−persistence\reflem:turning−birth−forces−adjacent−persistence.

That is the precise new frontier. If you want, I can now try to attack that lemma directly and see whether it really goes through or whether another obstruction appears.

I do not think that lemma is true as stated.

The proposed lemma was:

“Let Sigma_(m,b) be the set of indices j such that the line y = m x + b is 2-secant to branch I_j. If j is the leftmost index in Sigma and the boundary between I_j and I_(j+1) is a generic turning boundary, then j+1 is also in Sigma.”

That is too strong.

Why it fails:

What you currently know at a generic turning boundary is only a local exclusion of adjacent pair-pair. You do not know a global persistence law for fixed-slope intercept intervals. In support-interval language, for a fixed slope m, each branch has an interval B_j(m) of allowed intercepts. The new turning-boundary theorem says adjacent branches cannot both have b in the interior of their support intervals. But that does not force overlap to persist from B_j(m) into B_(j+1)(m).

Combinatorially, there is no contradiction in having, for one fixed slope m,

B_j(m) = (0,2),
B_(j+1)(m) = (3,4),
B_(j+2)(m) = (1,2).

Then b = 1.5 gives 2-secancy on j and j+2, but not on j+1. That already shows the persistence statement is not a consequence of “adjacent interiors are disjoint.”

And this is not just an abstract interval trick. Geometrically, you can realize that behavior with three strictly convex branches in x-order, joined by generic turning boundaries, where a line of fixed slope cuts the first and third branches twice but misses the middle branch as a 2-secant. Nothing in the current local turning-boundary theorem forbids that.

There is also a structural warning sign: if the lemma were true, then together with your new theorem excluding adjacent pair-pair across a generic turning boundary, you would immediately get something much stronger than you currently have — essentially a direct global no-four-secant result on any chain of turning boundaries — without needing the old support-ordering machinery. That is exactly the kind of “too good to be true” jump that usually signals the propagation lemma is overstated.

So I would not try to prove that lemma. I think the right conclusion is:

The “first birth persists into the next branch” statement is false, or at least far stronger than what the current ingredients justify.

What is true, and much closer to your actual machinery, is weaker:

If a line is 2-secant to two non-adjacent branches, then as you move from left to right across the chain of intervals, there is a first boundary where the fixed intercept b enters the support set. At that boundary, the entry cannot happen through a finite-order W=0, M ≠ 0 boundary by the local sterility theorem. So the first entry must occur at a generic turning boundary, and it occurs by tangential birth on one side, not necessarily by immediate 2-secancy on the next branch.

That weaker statement is exactly compatible with your current bridge lemmas:

    not “j+1 is also 2-secant,”

    but “at the first relevant turning boundary, one gets support contact / a directional kernel zero.”

That is a very different propagation mechanism.

So the corrected next target should be something like this:

For a fixed line y = m x + b, suppose it is 2-secant to two distinct branches, and choose such a pair with minimal index gap. Then there exists an internal generic turning boundary between them such that b lies in the support set of both adjacent branches at that boundary, with one side realizing b as an endpoint of its support interval. Equivalently, the overlap is born there by tangency, giving a directional kernel zero.

That statement is believable from the present ingredients. It matches:

    your finite-order W=0 sterility theorem,

    your compact support-overlap truncation lemma,

    your overlap-endpoint-to-directional-kernel-zero lemma,

But it does not give immediate adjacent 2-secancy.

So my math verdict is:

    the lemma you proposed is the wrong propagation lemma;

    the true propagation mechanism is “first entry into support at a turning boundary gives tangency / kernel zero,” not “first entry gives immediate 2-secancy on the next branch.”

That is the direction I would push now. It is also much more consistent with the current draft’s actual proven tools.

Fix a slope mm. For each branch IjIj​, define

first entry of b into support at a turning boundary⟹tangency / directional-kernel zero.

3. Determinant-kernel propagation also stalls with current inputs

The other natural route is to work directly with

is a scalar linear combination of c,v5,Δ7c,v5​,Δ7​, so on each ECT interval it has at most two zeros.

But that still does not give a global propagation law. A scalar combination can have two zeros on one interval, none on the next, then two again later, unless you know some stronger sign-regularity or oscillation theorem across boundaries.

4. So what would be a real global propagation statement?

At this point, I think the only plausible global bridge is a genuinely new theorem of one of these two forms:
A. A fixed-slope support-order theorem

For each finite slope mm, the support intervals Bj(m)Bj​(m) are globally ordered across the branch chain, so they cannot disappear and reappear.

This is conceptually enough, but right now it looks too strong to get from the present local ingredients alone.
B. A determinant mean-value / total-positivity theorem

Something like:

D(s,t,u)=21​W(ξ)(t−s)(u−s)(u−t)

for some intermediate ξξ, or at least a positive-kernel representation of DD in terms of WW.

Good news: the determinant mean-value theorem is actually true.

For the draft’s triple
F(h)=(c(h),v5(h),Δ7(h)),W(h)=det⁡(F(h),F′(h),F′′(h)),
F(h)=(c(h),v5​(h),Δ7​(h)),W(h)=det(F(h),F′(h),F′′(h)),

which is exactly the projective-Wronskian setup already isolated in the draft/convo, define
D(s,t,u):=det⁡(F(s),F(t),F(u)).
D(s,t,u):=det(F(s),F(t),F(u)).

Then for any distinct s,t,us,t,u in an interval where F∈C2F∈C2, there exists
ξ∈(min⁡{s,t,u},max⁡{s,t,u})
ξ∈(min{s,t,u},max{s,t,u})

such that
D(s,t,u)=12 W(ξ) (t−s)(u−s)(u−t).
D(s,t,u)=21​W(ξ)(t−s)(u−s)(u−t).

So the exact aggressive formula from convo4 is real.
Why it is true

Take the comparison triple
G(x)=(1,x,x22).
G(x)=(1,x,2x2​).

Then
det⁡(G(s),G(t),G(u))=12 (t−s)(u−s)(u−t),
det(G(s),G(t),G(u))=21​(t−s)(u−s)(u−t),

and
det⁡(G(x),G′(x),G′′(x))=1.
det(G(x),G′(x),G′′(x))=1.

Now apply the generalized Cauchy mean-value theorem for 3×33×3 determinants (the order-3 analogue of the ordinary scalar Cauchy mean-value theorem) to the two triples FF and GG. It gives some intermediate ξξ such that
det⁡(F(s),F(t),F(u))det⁡(G(s),G(t),G(u))=det⁡(F(ξ),F′(ξ),F′′(ξ))det⁡(G(ξ),G′(ξ),G′′(ξ)).
det(G(s),G(t),G(u))det(F(s),F(t),F(u))​=det(G(ξ),G′(ξ),G′′(ξ))det(F(ξ),F′(ξ),F′′(ξ))​.

Since the denominator on the right is 11, this becomes
D(s,t,u)=det⁡(F(s),F(t),F(u))=12 W(ξ) (t−s)(u−s)(u−t).

So the theorem is not special to the RH draft; it is a general order-3 determinant mean-value theorem for any C2C2 triple.
What this gives immediately

This gives an exact intervalwise discrete sign law:

if WW has fixed nonzero sign on the whole convex hull of {s,t,u}{s,t,u}, then
sgn⁡D(s,t,u)=sgn⁡W⋅sgn⁡((t−s)(u−s)(u−t)).
sgnD(s,t,u)=sgnW⋅sgn((t−s)(u−s)(u−t)).

This immediately kills:

    same-interval 3-secant lines,

    and therefore any same-interval 4-secant pathology.

What it does not yet give

It does not immediately solve the global problem.

The reason is that ξξ lies somewhere between s,t,us,t,u. If the ordered triple crosses a boundary where WW changes sign, the formula alone does not tell you which side ξξ lands on. So for triples spanning multiple WW-sign intervals, the sign of DD is still not controlled.

That means:

    the theorem solves the same-sign-hull case completely;

    the remaining issue is now sharply reduced to a cross-boundary sign-control problem for W(ξ)W(ξ).

This is exactly the “globalizing across adjacent boundaries” gap convo4 was pointing at.

    the determinant mean-value theorem is true;

    the only remaining obstacle is cross-boundary sign transport of the divided-difference kernel.

That is a much cleaner frontier than where we were before.

What this buys you

This is better than the pointwise mean-value theorem because it turns the global question into a variation-diminishing question for a positive kernel transform of WW.

The immediate consequences are:

    If WW has fixed sign on [s,u][s,u], then Q(s,t,u)Q(s,t,u) has that sign.

What I think is the next real theorem

The theorem to try now is not another boundary-local statement. It is:

    For fixed s<ts<t, the map u↦Q(s,t,u)u↦Q(s,t,u) is variation-diminishing with respect to the sign changes of WW.

In other words, if WW has only kk sign changes on [t,∞)[t,∞), then u↦Q(s,t,u)u↦Q(s,t,u) has at most kk sign changes on (t,∞)(t,∞).

If you could prove even a weak version of that, you would have a genuine global propagation statement.
The honest limitation

λ[μ1,μ2,μ3]=12 λ′′(η)
λ[μ1​,μ2​,μ3​]=21​λ′′(η)

for some intermediate ηη.

So the determinant sign problem is not a mysterious 3-vector problem anymore. It is just the sign of the second divided difference of the scalar function λ(μ)λ(μ).

That is the special structure you wanted.
5. What this suggests as the next real theorem

The obvious next target is no longer a theorem about WW directly. It is a theorem about the graph λ(μ)λ(μ).

A plausible statement is:

    On each connected interval where v5≠0v5​=0 and M≠0M=0, the normalized graph λ=λ(μ)λ=λ(μ) is strictly convex or strictly concave whenever WW has fixed sign.

That part is basically already proved by the identity above.

The harder global question then becomes:

    How do these convex/concave graph pieces glue across turning boundaries M=0M=0?

That is where the turning-boundary theorems you already proved come in. In the (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-picture, a turning boundary is exactly where μ′=0μ′=0, so the μμ-parameter breaks. That means the global obstruction is now very sharply localized:

    away from M=0M=0, the problem is ordinary graph convexity in (μ,λ)(μ,λ),

    at M=0M=0, the graph coordinate degenerates, and that is the only place where global propagation can really fail.

That is a much better reduction than the generic Peano-kernel view.
6. So what should we attack next?

The best next theorem to try is this:

    Turning-boundary compatibility of the (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-graph pieces.
    Show that if a finite-slope affine line in the original (ρ1,ρ2)(ρ1​,ρ2​)-chart meets two nonadjacent graph pieces λ=λ(μ)λ=λ(μ) twice each, then some adjacent turning-boundary pair already produces the same obstruction.

In plain language: convert the global problem into a statement about piecewise convex/concave graph pieces in the (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-plane, joined at turning points where μμ has an extremum.

That is now the most natural form of the remaining geometry.

What remains gauge-dependent is only the raw representative of A7fA7f​, not the canonical quotient class or Δ7Δ7​ itself.

So the (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-reformulation is even more justified now than that remark suggests.

4. The real special structure beyond generic convexity is Legendre-transform structure

Fix a branch IjIj​ on which λj(μ)λj​(μ) is strictly convex or strictly concave, so λj′(μ)λj′​(μ) is monotone.

For a slope bb in the interior tangent-slope range of that branch, let μj(b)μj​(b) be the unique point where
λj′(μj(b))=b.
λj′​(μj​(b))=b.

mj(b):=λj(μj(b))−b μj(b).
mj​(b):=λj​(μj​(b))−bμj​(b).

This is the intercept of the unique tangent line of slope bb to the branch.

Now differentiate. Standard Legendre-transform calculus gives
mj′(b)=−μj(b),mj′′(b)=−1λj′′(μj(b)).
mj′​(b)=−μj​(b),mj′′​(b)=−λj′′​(μj​(b))1​.

This is the extra structure that generic positive-kernel arguments do not see.

So:

    on a convex branch λj′′>0λj′′​>0, the tangent-intercept function mj(b)mj​(b) is strictly concave;

    on a concave branch λj′′<0λj′′​<0, mj(b)mj​(b) is strictly convex.

And a line of slope bb is 2-secant to branch jj exactly when its intercept mm lies on the correct side of mj(b)mj​(b):

    above mj(b)mj​(b) for convex pieces,

    below mj(b)mj​(b) for concave pieces.

So the whole global problem is now equivalent to understanding the family of scalar functions
b⟼mj(b)
b⟼mj​(b)

for the different graph pieces.

That is the strongest reduction I can see.
5. What this immediately explains

It explains why the local turning-boundary theorem works.

At a generic turning boundary, the two adjacent graph pieces meet at a common endpoint (μb,λb)(μb​,λb​) with vertical tangent. Locally they have asymptotics
λ−λb∼±Cμ−μb
λ−λb​∼±Cμ−μb​
​

or the reflected variant, so their tangent-intercept functions have the same leading affine part
mj(b)∼λb−bμb
mj​(b)∼λb​−bμb​

but opposite 1/b1/b-corrections. That gives local separation of the two tangent envelopes, which is the geometric content of your adjacent turning-boundary exclusion theorem.

So the new theorem is sitting exactly where the (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-picture says it should.
6. Where the attack stalls

The key global question is now:

    Are the functions mj(b)mj​(b) globally ordered in jj for each fixed slope bb?

If they were, then nonadjacent pair-pair would be impossible, because a fixed intercept mm could not lie on the “2-secant side” of two separated tangent-envelope functions without also doing so for an intermediate one.

But this is exactly where the current ingredients stop.

From
mj′(b)=−μj(b)
mj′​(b)=−μj​(b)

you see that global ordering of the mjmj​ would follow from global ordering of the tangent locations μj(b)μj​(b) across branches.

And that is the actual missing theorem.

Right now, nothing in the draft proves that for fixed slope bb,
μ1(b),μ2(b),μ3(b),…
μ1​(b),μ2​(b),μ3​(b),…

are monotone in the branch index or even ordered enough to force the mjmj​ to be ordered.

Without that, you can still have the same slope bb appearing on nonadjacent convex/concave pieces with tangent-intercept functions that dip and rise again. So the global no-four-secant theorem still does not fall out.

In other words:
What the (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-attack has genuinely achieved

It has reduced the whole remaining global problem to a family of one-variable convex/concave functions mj(b)mj​(b).
What it has not yet achieved

It has not proved the global ordering theorem for those mjmj​.
7. So the next precise theorem to attack is now very clear

Not another boundary-local theorem. Not another kernel-positivity theorem.

The exact next target is:

    For each fixed slope bb, prove an ordering theorem for the tangent locations μj(b)μj​(b), or equivalently for the tangent-intercept functions mj(b)mj​(b), across the branch chain.

That would be enough to globalize.

A slightly weaker but still useful version would be:

    For each fixed slope bb, the functions mj(b)mj​(b) cannot leave and later re-enter the same side of a given intercept level mm without an adjacent turning-boundary violation.

That would also do the job.

So the attack did not close the proof, but it did isolate the global obstruction extremely cleanly:
Global no-four-secant  ⟺  global ordering/variation control of the tangent-intercept functions mj(b).
Global no-four-secant⟺global ordering/variation control of the tangent-intercept functions mj​(b).​

That is much sharper than anything we had before.

ok attack

I attacked the (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-route directly, and there is a real theorem there — but it also shows exactly where the remaining global gap sits.

The useful global object is this:

On each branch IjIj​ where
v5≠0,M≠0,
v5​=0,M=0,

define
μ=cv5,λ=Δ7v5.
μ=v5​c​,λ=v5​Δ7​​.

Then μμ is a valid parameter on that branch, so the normalized curve becomes a graph

dμ2d2λ​=M3v53​​W,

so on each branch where WW keeps sign, λjλj​ is strictly convex or strictly concave.

Now fix a slope bb in the (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-plane. The tangent-intercept function of branch jj is
mj(b):=λj(μj(b))−b μj(b),
mj​(b):=λj​(μj​(b))−bμj​(b),

where μj(b)μj​(b) is the unique point on branch jj with
λj′(μj(b))=b.
λj′​(μj​(b))=b.

This is the right global object, because a line
λ=b μ+m
λ=bμ+m

is 22-secant to branch jj exactly when mm lies on the correct side of mj(b)mj​(b):

    if λjλj​ is convex, then 22-secant means m>mj(b)m>mj​(b);

    if λjλj​ is concave, then 22-secant means m<mj(b)m<mj​(b).

So the full global no-four-secant problem becomes a statement about the family of one-variable functions mj(b)mj​(b).

That is the good reduction.

Now here is the theorem you can prove from the current ingredients.
Adjacent envelope separation theorem

Let Ij<Ij+1Ij​<Ij+1​ be adjacent across a generic turning boundary, and suppose both branches admit the same tangent slope bb in the (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-plane. Then their tangent-intercept functions are strictly ordered:

    if IjIj​ is convex and Ij+1Ij+1​ is concave, then
    mj(b)>mj+1(b);
    mj​(b)>mj+1​(b);

    if IjIj​ is concave and Ij+1Ij+1​ is convex, then
    mj(b)<mj+1(b).
    mj​(b)<mj+1​(b).

Why this is true

Take the first case, convex left / concave right. If instead
mj(b)≤mj+1(b),
mj​(b)≤mj+1​(b),

then choose any intercept mm strictly between them:
mj(b)<m<mj+1(b)ormj(b)=mj+1(b)=m.
mj​(b)<m<mj+1​(b)ormj​(b)=mj+1​(b)=m.

For the convex branch IjIj​, a line λ=bμ+mλ=bμ+m with m>mj(b)m>mj​(b) is 22-secant. For the concave branch Ij+1Ij+1​, a line with m<mj+1(b)m<mj+1​(b) is 22-secant. So the same line would be 22-secant to both adjacent branches, contradicting your new unconditional adjacent pair-pair exclusion theorem across generic turning boundaries.

So the inequality must be strict. The other case is the mirror image.

This is a genuine global-looking statement extracted from the current local theorem.

It tells you that adjacent turning boundaries impose strict ordering of tangent envelopes in (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-space.

Now for the important part:
Why this still does not globalize by itself

The family mj(b)mj​(b) now satisfies adjacent inequalities of the form
m1(b)>m2(b)<m3(b)>m4(b)<⋯
m1​(b)>m2​(b)<m3​(b)>m4​(b)<⋯

or the reversed pattern, depending on convexity parity.

But this alternating adjacent order is not enough to rule out nonadjacent pair-pair.

A simple abstract example shows the issue:
m1(b)=2,m2(b)=0,m3(b)=2.
m1​(b)=2,m2​(b)=0,m3​(b)=2.

Then the adjacent inequalities are satisfied:
m1>m2,m3>m2,
m1​>m2​,m3​>m2​,

but any line with intercept m=1m=1 is still on the 22-secant side of branches 11 and 33, while not being 22-secant to branch 22.

So from the current ingredients, you get local adjacent envelope separation, but not yet a monotone chain ordering.

That means the real remaining global theorem is now very sharply isolated:

    for each fixed bb, prove a global ordering / no-reentry theorem for the tangent-intercept functions mj(b)mj​(b).

Equivalently:

    either show the functions mj(b)mj​(b) cannot dip and then rise again as jj varies,

    or show the tangent locations μj(b)μj​(b) are ordered strongly enough that mj′(b)=−μj(b)mj′​(b)=−μj​(b) forces global ordering.

At this point, that theorem does not follow from what is already in the draft.

So the attack gives a mixed result:
What we gained

We reduced the whole remaining global geometry to a family of scalar envelope functions mj(b)mj​(b), and proved a strict adjacent separation theorem for them across generic turning boundaries.
What remains open

We still do not have a global no-reentry theorem for those envelopes.

And I think that is now the cleanest statement of the remaining gap.

So the honest “attack result” is:

    the (μ,λ)(μ,λ)-reformulation is definitely the right language;

    the unconditional adjacent turning theorem translates into a strict adjacent ordering theorem for tangent envelopes;

    but the missing global step is now exactly:
    prove that the envelope family mj(b) cannot re-enter globally.
    prove that the envelope family mj​(b) cannot re-enter globally.​

That is the next real theorem to target.

mj(b):=λj(μj(b))−b μj(b),

The proof is clean. If the inequality failed, then choose an intercept mm between the two tangent intercepts. But this still does not globalize.

The reason is simple and important: adjacent strict inequalities do not prevent nonadjacent re-entry. Abstractly, you can have

both hold, but any line with intercept m=1m=1 is still on the 22-secant side of branches 11 and 33, while not being 22-secant to branch 22. So the sought global theorem
“the envelope family mj(b) cannot leave and later re-enter”
“the envelope family mj​(b) cannot leave and later re-enter”

does not follow from the current local ingredients.

So the honest outcome of the attack is:

    proved: adjacent turning boundaries impose strict adjacent ordering of the tangent-envelope functions mj(b)mj​(b);

    not proved: global no-reentry of the family mj(b)mj​(b).

That means the real remaining theorem is now extremely sharply isolated:

Because

mj′​(b)=−μj​(b),

a global ordering theorem for the μj(b)μj​(b) would force a global ordering theorem for the mj(b)mj​(b). But nothing in the current draft/convo yet gives that. The current material really does stop at adjacent exclusion and local geometric harmlessness of the two generic boundary types.

a self-intersection of the dual curve between same-parity arcs produces a pair-pair line.​

(b,m)=(0,0).

    Stop trying to globalize purely geometrically and return to a more algebraic obstruction — something special about the actual Δ7Δ7​, WW, or ββ that has no analogue in the toy countermodel.

So this attack was still useful: it shows the remaining gap is not just a missing argument. It is a genuinely missing global special-structure theorem.

can we deeply consider all 3 options?

I’m pulling the exact current-draft anchors for the three live fronts — the W=0W=0 reductions, the new unconditional adjacent turning theorem, and the “current state/bottleneck” remarks — so I can evaluate the options against what the paper now actually has, not just our running memory.

Yes. I think there are really three live options, and they are now different enough that it is worth treating them separately rather than lumping them together as “the remaining geometry.”

The right way to think about them is:

    Option 1 tries to prove a global scalar monotonicity / non-recurrence theorem.

    Option 2 tries to prove a global arrangement theorem for the turning-boundary seams.

    Option 3 accepts that pure geometry may not close and goes back to find a special algebraic obstruction in the actual RH package.

I’ll go through each carefully, say what it would need, what the draft already gives, where it is likely to fail, and how I would rank it.
Option 1: control the scalar slope function ββ

This is the cleanest reduction we have so far.

On a region where v5≠0v5​=0 and M≠0M=0, define

β(h):=dλdμ(h).
β(h):=dμdλ​(h).

and, crucially,
β′(h)=−v5(h)M(h)2 W(h).
β′(h)=−M(h)2v5​(h)​W(h).

So the global line-incidence problem has been reduced to a scalar question:

    can β(h)β(h) leave a level bb and later return to it on a nonadjacent branch?

Because tangent points of slope bb are exactly solutions of
β(h)=b.
β(h)=b.
What this option would need

It would need a theorem that rules out that kind of recurrence. Any of the following would be enough:

    v5Wv5​W has one sign on a whole generic component;

    v5Wv5​W changes sign at most once;

    ββ is globally unimodal or piecewise monotone in a one-way fashion;

    level sets of ββ cannot reappear after a generic turning seam.

Any one of those would globalize the local theorems.
What the draft already gives

The draft now gives all the exact identities needed to make this scalar reduction meaningful:

    W≡0W≡0 on an M≠0M=0 interval is excluded under the septic non-affinity hypothesis;

    finite-order W=0, M≠0W=0, M=0 gives only ordinary inflection behavior and is locally sterile;

    generic turning boundaries are locally excluded for adjacent pair-pair;

    the order-7 package has largely shifted from “find a detector” to “understand the canonical Δ7Δ7​-geometry.”

So this option is no longer blocked by missing notation. It is blocked by a genuinely new theorem.
Why I think it is attractive

It is the cleanest remaining formulation. It takes the global problem out of “support overlap” language and puts it into the scalar ODE-like identity
β′=−v5M2W.
β′=−M2v5​​W.

That is mathematically sharp.
Why I think it is hard

Right now there is no evidence in the draft that v5Wv5​W has global sign control. The current local results tell you what happens between zeros and at individual boundary types, but they do not stop WW or v5Wv5​W from changing sign several times.

So if this option is going to work, it needs a truly new special-structure theorem, not just more local geometry.
Best sub-attacks inside Option 1

The best concrete sub-questions are:

    Can v5v5​ be shown to have fixed sign on a generic component?

    Can WW be shown to have at most one sign change on such a component?

    Can ββ be shown to be a solution of a scalar linear ODE with disconjugacy properties?

My verdict: conceptually best, but currently the most speculative.
Option 2: global arrangement of turning-boundary seams / dual arcs
